<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="background: #f8eaba; text-align: center;">Hazards<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_card_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_card-12">[12]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PubChem_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-PubChem-13">[13]</a></sup>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Main <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worker_safety_and_health" title="Worker safety and health">hazards</a>
</td>
<td>Moderately <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">Toxic</a> for small animals – Highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">Toxic</a> to large animals and humans — May be fatal/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lethal" title="Lethal">lethal</a> or cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blindness" title="Blindness">blindness</a> and damage to the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidneys" title="Kidneys">kidneys</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> if swallowed – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">Toxic</a> effects from repeated over exposure have an accumulative effect on the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Optic_nerve" title="Optic nerve">optic nerve</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symptoms" title="Symptoms">Symptoms</a> may be delayed, become severe after 12 to 18 hours, and linger for several days after exposure <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-7">[7]</a></sup>
</td></tr>

Do NOT induce vomiting">P301+330+331</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Immediately call a POISON CENTER or doctor/physician">P310</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="IF ON SKIN: Wash with soap and water">P302+352</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Call a POISON CENTER or doctor/physician if you feel unwell">P312</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="IF ON SKIN (or hair): Remove/Take off immediately all contaminated clothing. Rinse skin with water/shower">P303+361+353</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="IF INHALED: Remove victim to fresh air and keep at rest in a position comfortable for breathing">P304+340</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Call a POISON CENTER or doctor/physician">P311</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="IF IN EYES: Rinse continuously with water for several minutes. Remove contact lenses if present and easy to do – continue rinsing">P305+351+338</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="IF exposed: Call a POISON CENTER or doctor/physician">P307+311</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Get medical advice/attention">P337+313</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Remove/Take off immediately all contaminated clothing">P361</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Wash contaminated clothing before reuse">P363</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="In case of fire: Use … for extinction">P370+378</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Store in a well ventilated place. <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><img alt="☒" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="525" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/X_mark.svg/12px-X_mark.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/X_mark.svg/18px-X_mark.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/X_mark.svg/24px-X_mark.svg.png 2x" width="12"/><span style="display:none">N</span> <span class="reflink plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:ComparePages&amp;rev1=409752739&amp;page2=Methanol">verify</a></span> (<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Chemicals/Chembox_validation" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Chemicals/Chembox validation">what is</a> <sup><img alt="☑" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="7" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/Yes_check.svg/7px-Yes_check.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/Yes_check.svg/11px-Yes_check.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/Yes_check.svg/14px-Yes_check.svg.png 2x" width="7"/><span style="display:none">Y</span><img alt="☒" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="525" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/X_mark.svg/7px-X_mark.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/X_mark.svg/11px-X_mark.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/X_mark.svg/14px-X_mark.svg.png 2x" width="7"/><span style="display:none">N</span></sup> ?)
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#f8eaba; border-top:2px solid transparent;">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Chemical_infobox#References" title="Wikipedia:Chemical infobox">Infobox references</a>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;">
</td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Methanol</b>, also known as <b>methyl alcohol</b> among others, is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">formula</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">H</a><sub>3</sub><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">H</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Methyl_group" title="Methyl group">methyl group</a> linked to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_group" title="Hydroxyl group">hydroxyl group</a>, often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbreviated" title="Abbreviated">abbreviated</a> MeOH). Methanol acquired the name <b>wood alcohol</b> because it was once produced chiefly by the <a href="/wiki/Destructive_distillation" title="Destructive distillation">destructive distillation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>.  Today, methanol is mainly produced industrially by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methanol is the simplest <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a>, consisting of a <a href="/wiki/Methyl_group" title="Methyl group">methyl group</a> linked to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> group. It is a light, <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a>, colorless, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flammable" title="Flammable">flammable</a> liquid with a distinctive odor similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> (drinking alcohol).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ershdb_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-ershdb-16">[16]</a></sup> Methanol is however far more toxic than ethanol. At room temperature, it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_solvent" title="Polar solvent">polar liquid</a>. With more than 20 million tons produced annually, it is used as a precursor to other <a href="/wiki/Commodity_chemicals" title="Commodity chemicals">commodity chemicals</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methyl_tert-butyl_ether" title="Methyl tert-butyl ether">methyl tert-butyl ether</a>, as well as a host of more specialized chemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Small amounts of methanol are found in normal, healthy human individuals, concluded by one study which found a mean of 4.5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> in the exhaled breath of subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The mean endogenous methanol in humans of 0.45 g/d may be metabolized from <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a> found in fruit; one kilogram of apple produces up to 1.4 g methanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methanol is produced naturally in the <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic</a> <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> of many varieties of bacteria and is commonly present in small amounts in the environment. As a result, the atmosphere contains a small amount of methanol vapor. Atmospheric methanol is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidized" title="Oxidized">oxidized</a> by air in sunlight to carbon dioxide and water over the course of days.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_medium">Interstellar medium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Interstellar medium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Methanol is also found in abundant quantities in star-forming regions of space and is used in astronomy as a marker for such regions. It is detected through its spectral emission lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, astronomers using the <a href="/wiki/MERLIN" title="MERLIN">MERLIN</a> array of radio telescopes at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jodrell_Bank" title="Jodrell Bank">Jodrell Bank Observatory</a> discovered a large cloud of methanol in space, 288 million miles (463 million km) across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In 2016, astronomers detected methyl alcohol in a planet-forming disc around the young star <a href="/wiki/TW_Hydrae" title="TW Hydrae">TW Hydrae</a> using <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Large_Millimeter_Array" title="Atacama Large Millimeter Array">ALMA</a> radio telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Methanol_toxicity" title="Methanol toxicity">Methanol toxicity</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_methanol_poisoning_incidents" title="List of methanol poisoning incidents">List of methanol poisoning incidents</a></div>
<p>Methanol has low acute toxicity in humans but is dangerous because, together with ethanol, it is occasionally ingested in large volumes. As little as 10 mL (0.34 US fl oz) of pure methanol can cause permanent blindness by destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Optic_nerve" title="Optic nerve">optic nerve</a>.  30 mL (1.0 US fl oz) is potentially fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vale_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vale-23">[23]</a></sup>  The median lethal dose is 100 mL (3.4 US fl oz) (i.e. 1–2 mL/kg body weight of pure methanol<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>). The <a href="/wiki/Reference_dose" title="Reference dose">reference dose</a> for methanol is 2 mg/kg in a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Toxic effects begin hours after ingestion, and antidotes can often prevent permanent damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vale_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vale-23">[23]</a></sup> Because of its similarities in both appearance and odor to <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> (the alcohol in beverages), it is difficult to differentiate between the two (such is also the case with <a href="/wiki/Denatured_alcohol" title="Denatured alcohol">denatured alcohol</a>, adulterated liquors or very low quality alcoholic beverages). However, cases exist of methanol resistance, such as that of <a href="/wiki/Michael_Malloy" title="Michael Malloy">Mike Malloy</a> who was the victim of a failed murder attempt by methanol in the early 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blum2011_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blum2011-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methanol is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">toxic</a> by two mechanisms. First, methanol can be fatal due to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CNS_depressant" title="CNS depressant">CNS depressant</a> properties in the same manner as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethanol_poisoning" title="Ethanol poisoning">ethanol poisoning</a>. Second, in a process of <a href="/wiki/Toxication" title="Toxication">toxication</a>, it is <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolized</a> to formic acid (which is present as the formate ion) via <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> in a process initiated by the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dehydrogenase" title="Alcohol dehydrogenase">alcohol dehydrogenase</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schep_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schep-28">[28]</a></sup> Methanol is converted to formaldehyde via alcohol dehydrogenase (ADH) and formaldehyde is converted to formic acid (formate) via <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde_dehydrogenase" title="Aldehyde dehydrogenase">aldehyde dehydrogenase</a> (ALDH).  The conversion to formate via ALDH proceeds completely, with no detectable formaldehyde remaining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noCHOH_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-noCHOH-29">[29]</a></sup> Formate is toxic because it inhibits mitochondrial <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_c_oxidase" title="Cytochrome c oxidase">cytochrome c oxidase</a>, causing <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a> at the cellular level, and <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_acidosis" title="Metabolic acidosis">metabolic acidosis</a>, among a variety of other metabolic disturbances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Outbreak" title="Outbreak">Outbreaks</a> of methanol poisoning have occurred due to contamination of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">drinking alcohol</a>. This is more common in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EM2016_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-EM2016-31">[31]</a></sup> In 2013 more than 1700 cases occurred in the United States. Those affected are often adult men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fer2016_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fer2016-32">[32]</a></sup> Outcomes may be good with early treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kr2012_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kr2012-33">[33]</a></sup> Toxicity to methanol was described as early as 1856.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of its toxic properties, methanol is frequently used as a denaturant additive for ethanol manufactured for industrial uses. This addition of methanol exempts industrial ethanol (commonly known as "<a href="/wiki/Denatured_alcohol" title="Denatured alcohol">denatured alcohol</a>" or "methylated spirit") from liquor <a href="/wiki/Excise_tax_in_the_United_States" title="Excise tax in the United States">excise taxation</a> in the US and some other countries.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Heavy_chemicals:_formaldehyde.2C_acetic_acid.2C_methyl_tert-butylether"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Heavy_chemicals:_formaldehyde,_acetic_acid,_methyl_tert-butylether">Heavy chemicals: formaldehyde, acetic acid, methyl tert-butylether</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Heavy chemicals: formaldehyde, acetic acid, methyl tert-butylether">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Methanol is primarily converted to <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, which is widely used in many areas, especially polymers. The conversion entails oxidation:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 CH<sub>3</sub>OH  +  O<sub>2</sub>   →   2 CH<sub>2</sub>O  +  2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">Acetic acid</a> can be produced from methanol.
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cativa-process-catalytic-cycle.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1887" data-file-width="2150" height="351" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Cativa-process-catalytic-cycle.png/400px-Cativa-process-catalytic-cycle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Cativa-process-catalytic-cycle.png/600px-Cativa-process-catalytic-cycle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Cativa-process-catalytic-cycle.png/800px-Cativa-process-catalytic-cycle.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cativa-process-catalytic-cycle.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Cativa_process" title="Cativa process">Cativa process</a> converts methanol into <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cativa_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cativa-35">[35]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Methanol and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isobutene" title="Isobutene">isobutene</a> are combined to give <a href="/wiki/Methyl_tert-butyl_ether" title="Methyl tert-butyl ether">methyl tert-butyl ether</a> (MTBE). MTBE is a major octane booster in gasoline.
</p>
<h3><span id="Methanol_to_hydrocarbons.2C_olefins.2C_gasoline"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Methanol_to_hydrocarbons,_olefins,_gasoline">Methanol to hydrocarbons, olefins, gasoline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Methanol to hydrocarbons, olefins, gasoline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Condensation of methanol to produce <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic</a> systems is the basis of several technologies related to <a href="/wiki/Gas_to_liquids" title="Gas to liquids">gas to liquids</a>. These include methanol-to-hydrocarbons (MTH), methanol to gasoline (MTG), and methanol to olefins (MTO), and methanol to propylene (MTP).  These conversions are catalyzed by <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">zeolites</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterogeneous_catalyst" title="Heterogeneous catalyst">heterogeneous catalysts</a>. The MTG process was once commercialized at <a href="/wiki/Motunui" title="Motunui">Motunui</a> in New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gasoline_additive">Gasoline additive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Gasoline additive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=European_Fuel_Quality_Directive&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="European Fuel Quality Directive (page does not exist)">European Fuel Quality Directive</a> allows up to 3% methanol with an equal amount of cosolvent to be blended with gasoline sold in Europe. <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> uses more than one billion gallons of methanol per year as a transportation fuel in low level blends for conventional vehicles and high level blends in vehicles designed for methanol fuels.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_chemicals">Other chemicals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other chemicals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Methanol is the precursor to most simple <a href="/wiki/Methylamine" title="Methylamine">methylamines</a>, methyl halides, methyl ethers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-15">[15]</a></sup>  Methyl esters are produced from methanol, including the transesterification of fats and production of <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> via <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Niche_and_potential_uses">Niche and potential uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Niche and potential uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_carrier">Energy carrier</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Energy carrier">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Methanol is a promising <a href="/wiki/Energy_carrier" title="Energy carrier">energy carrier</a> because, as a liquid, it is easier to store than hydrogen and natural gas.  Its <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> is however low reflecting the fact that it represents partially combusted methane.  Its energy density is 15.6 <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">MJ</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liter" title="Liter">L</a>, whereas ethanol's is 24 and gasoline's is 33 MJ/L.
</p><p>Further advantages for methanol is its ready biodegradability and low toxicity. It does not persist in either aerobic (oxygen-present) or anaerobic (oxygen-absent) environments. The half-life for methanol in groundwater is just one to seven days, while many common gasoline components have half-lives in the hundreds of days (such as <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> at 10–730 days). Since methanol is <a href="/wiki/Miscibility" title="Miscibility">miscible</a> with water and biodegradable, it is unlikely to accumulate in groundwater, surface water, air or soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_for_vehicles">Fuel for vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fuel for vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Methanol_fuel" title="Methanol fuel">Methanol fuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methanol_economy" title="Methanol economy">methanol economy</a></div>
<p>Methanol is occasionally used to fuel <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a>. It burns forming carbon dioxide and water:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 CH<sub>3</sub>OH + 3 O<sub>2</sub> → 2 CO<sub>2</sub> + 4 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>One problem with high concentrations of methanol in fuel is that alcohols <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrode</a> some metals, particularly <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>. Methanol fuel has been proposed for ground transportation. The chief advantage of a methanol economy is that it could be adapted to gasoline internal combustion engines with minimum modification to the engines and to the infrastructure that delivers and stores liquid fuel.  Its energy density is however only half that of gasoline, meaning that twice the volume of methanol would be required.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_applications">Other applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Other applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Methanol is a traditional denaturant for ethanol, the product being known as "<a href="/wiki/Denatured_alcohol" title="Denatured alcohol">denatured alcohol</a>" or "methylated spirit". This was commonly used during the <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">Prohibition</a> to discourage consumption of <a href="/wiki/Moonshine" title="Moonshine">bootlegged</a> liquor, and ended up causing several deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methanol is used as a <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> and as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antifreeze_(coolant)" title="Antifreeze (coolant)">antifreeze</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Windshield_washer_fluid" title="Windshield washer fluid">windshield washer fluid</a>.  Methanol was used as an automobile coolant antifreeze in the early 1900s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">wastewater treatment plants</a>, a small amount of methanol is added to <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> to provide a carbon food source for the <a href="/wiki/Denitrifying_bacteria" title="Denitrifying bacteria">denitrifying bacteria</a>, which convert <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> gas and reduce the nitrification of sensitive <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>.
</p><p>Methanol is used as a destaining agent in <a href="/wiki/Polyacrylamide_gel_electrophoresis" title="Polyacrylamide gel electrophoresis">polyacrylamide gel electrophoresis</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct-methanol_fuel_cell" title="Direct-methanol fuel cell">Direct-methanol fuel cells</a> are unique in their low temperature, atmospheric pressure operation, allowing them to be miniaturized to an unprecedented degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> This, combined with the relatively easy and safe storage and handling of methanol, may open the possibility of fuel cell-powered <a href="/wiki/Consumer_electronics" title="Consumer electronics">consumer electronics</a>, such as laptop computers and mobile phones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methanol is also a widely used fuel in camping and boating stoves. Methanol burns well in an unpressurized burner, so alcohol stoves are often very simple, sometimes little more than a cup to hold fuel. This lack of complexity makes them a favorite of hikers who spend extended time in the wilderness.  Similarly, the alcohol can be gelled to reduce risk of leaking or spilling, as with the brand "<a href="/wiki/Sterno" title="Sterno">Sterno</a>".
</p><p>Methanol is mixed with water and injected into high performance diesel and gasoline engines for an increase of power and a decrease in intake air temperature in a process known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_injection_(engines)" title="Water injection (engines)">water methanol injection</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_synthesis_gas">From synthesis gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: From synthesis gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon monoxide and hydrogen react over a catalyst to produce methanol. Today, the most widely used catalyst is a mixture of copper and <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">zinc oxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalyst_support" title="Catalyst support">supported</a> on alumina, as first used by <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">ICI</a> in 1966. At 5–10 MPa (50–100 atm) and 250 °C (482 °F), the reaction is characterized by high selectivity (&gt;99.8%):
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + 2 H<sub>2</sub> → CH<sub>3</sub>OH</dd></dl>
<p>The production of synthesis gas from methane produces three <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">moles</a> of hydrogen for every mole of carbon monoxide, whereas the synthesis consumes only two moles of hydrogen gas per mole of carbon monoxide. One way of dealing with the excess hydrogen is to inject <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> into the methanol synthesis reactor, where it, too, reacts to form methanol according to the equation:
</p>
<dl><dd>CO<sub>2</sub>  +  3 H<sub>2</sub>   →   CH<sub>3</sub>OH  +  H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>In terms of mechanism, the process occurs via initial conversion of CO into CO<sub>2</sub>, which is then <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenated</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>CO<sub>2</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub> → CH<sub>3</sub>OH + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>where the H<sub>2</sub>O byproduct is recycled via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" title="Water gas shift reaction">water-gas shift reaction</a>
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + H<sub>2</sub>O → CO<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>,</dd></dl>
<p>This gives an overall reaction, which is the same as listed above.
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + 2 H<sub>2</sub> → CH<sub>3</sub>OH</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biosynthesis">Biosynthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Biosynthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The catalytic conversion of methane to methanol is effected by enzymes including <a href="/wiki/Methane_monooxygenase" title="Methane monooxygenase">methane monooxygenases</a>.  These enzymes are mixed-function oxygenases, i.e. oxygenation is coupled with production of water:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>CH<sub>4</sub>  +  O<sub>2</sub>  +  NADPH  +  H<sup>+</sup>  →  CH<sub>3</sub>OH  +  H<sub>2</sub>O  +  NAD<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>Both Fe- and Cu-dependent enzymes have been characterized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>  Intense but largely fruitless efforts have been undertaken to emulate this reactivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alayon_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alayon-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Catalytic_and_Mechanic_Insights_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Catalytic_and_Mechanic_Insights-50">[50]</a></sup>  Methanol is more easily oxidized than is the feedstock methane, so the reactions tend not to be selective. Some strategies exist to circumvent this problem. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Shilov_system" title="Shilov system">Shilov systems</a> and Fe- and Cu containing zeolites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> These systems do not necessarily mimick the mechanisms employed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metalloenzymes" title="Metalloenzymes">metalloenzymes</a>, but draw some inspiration from them. Active sites can vary substantially from those known in the enzymes. For example, a dinuclear active site is proposed in the <a href="/wiki/Methane_monooxygenase" title="Methane monooxygenase">sMMO</a> enzyme, whereas a mononuclear iron (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha-Oxygen" title="Alpha-Oxygen">alpha-Oxygen</a>) is proposed in the Fe-zeolite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quality_specifications_and_analysis">Quality specifications and analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Quality specifications and analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Methanol is available commercially in various purity grades.  Commercial methanol is generally classified according to ASTM purity grades A and AA. Methanol for chemical use normally corresponds to Grade AA. In addition to water, typical impurities include acetone and ethanol (which are very difficult to separate by distillation). UV-vis spectroscopy is a convenient method for detecting aromatic impurities. Water content can be determined by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Karl-Fischer_titration&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Karl-Fischer titration (page does not exist)">Karl-Fischer titration</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In their <a href="/wiki/Embalming" title="Embalming">embalming</a> process, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptians" title="Ancient Egyptians">ancient Egyptians</a> used a mixture of substances, including methanol, which they obtained from the <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> of wood. Pure methanol, however, was first isolated in 1661 by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>, when he produced it via the distillation of <a href="/wiki/Buxus" title="Buxus">buxus</a> (boxwood).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> It later became known as "pyroxylic spirit". In 1834, the French chemists <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Dumas" title="Jean-Baptiste Dumas">Jean-Baptiste Dumas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugene_Peligot" title="Eugene Peligot">Eugene Peligot</a> determined its elemental composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumas_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dumas-54">[54]</a></sup>
They also introduced the word "methylène" to organic chemistry, forming it from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>methy</i> = "alcoholic liquid" + <i>hȳlē</i> = "woodland, forest", with a Greek language error: <i>xylon</i> = "wood as a material" would have been more suitable. "Methylène" designated a <a href="/wiki/Radical_theory" title="Radical theory">"radical"</a> that was about 14% hydrogen by weight and contained one carbon atom. This would be CH<sub>2</sub>, but at the time carbon was thought to have an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> only six times that of hydrogen, so they gave the formula as CH.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumas_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dumas-54">[54]</a></sup> They then called wood alcohol (l'esprit de bois) "bihydrate de méthylène" (bihydrate because they thought the formula was C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>4</sub> = (CH)<sub>4</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>). The term "methyl" was derived in about 1840 by <a href="/wiki/Back-formation" title="Back-formation">back-formation</a> from "methylene", and was then applied to describe "methyl alcohol". This was shortened to "methanol" in 1892 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature" title="IUPAC nomenclature">International Conference on Chemical Nomenclature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>  The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suffix_(linguistics)" title="Suffix (linguistics)">suffix</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/-yl" title="-yl">-yl</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a> to form names of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> groups, was extracted from the word "methyl".
</p><p>In 1923, the German chemists <a href="/wiki/Alwin_Mittasch" title="Alwin Mittasch">Alwin Mittasch</a> and Mathias Pier, working for <a href="/wiki/BASF" title="BASF">Badische-Anilin &amp; Soda-Fabrik</a> (BASF), developed a means to convert <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">synthesis gas</a> (a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>) into methanol. US patent 1,569,775 (<span class="citation patent"><a class="external text" href="https://worldwide.espacenet.com/textdoc?DB=EPODOC&amp;IDX=US1569775" rel="nofollow">US 1569775</a></span><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Apatent&amp;rft.number=1569775&amp;rft.cc=US&amp;rft.title="><span style="display: none;"> </span></span>) was applied for on 4 Sep 1924 and issued on 12 January 1926; the process used a <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manganese_oxide" title="Manganese oxide">manganese oxide</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> with extremely vigorous conditions: pressures ranging from 50 to 220 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atm</a>, and temperatures up to 450 °C. Modern methanol production has been made more efficient through use of catalysts (commonly copper) capable of operating at lower pressures. The modern low pressure methanol (LPM) process was developed by <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">ICI</a> in the late 1960s <span class="citation patent"><a class="external text" href="https://worldwide.espacenet.com/textdoc?DB=EPODOC&amp;IDX=US3326956" rel="nofollow">US 3326956</a></span><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Apatent&amp;rft.number=3326956&amp;rft.cc=US&amp;rft.title="><span style="display: none;"> </span></span> with the technology now owned by <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Matthey" title="Johnson Matthey">Johnson Matthey</a>, which is a leading licensor of methanol technology.
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, methanol was used as a fuel in several German military rocket designs, under the name M-Stoff, and in a roughly 50/50 mixture with <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/C-Stoff" title="C-Stoff">C-Stoff</a>.
</p><p>The use of methanol as a motor fuel received attention during the <a href="/wiki/1970s_energy_crisis" title="1970s energy crisis">oil crises of the 1970s</a>. By the mid-1990s, over 20,000 methanol "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flexible_fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible fuel vehicle">flexible fuel vehicles</a>" capable of operating on methanol or gasoline were introduced in the U.S. In addition, low levels of methanol were blended in gasoline fuels sold in Europe during much of the 1980s and early-1990s. Automakers stopped building methanol FFVs by the late-1990s, switching their attention to ethanol-fueled vehicles. While the methanol FFV program was a technical success, rising methanol pricing in the mid- to late-1990s during a period of slumping gasoline pump prices diminished interest in methanol fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1970s, a process was developed by <a href="/wiki/Mobil" title="Mobil">Mobil</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methanol_to_gasoline" title="Methanol to gasoline">producing gasoline</a> fuel from methanol.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Between the 1960s and 1980s methanol emerged as a precursor to the feedstock chemicals <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetic_anhydride" title="Acetic anhydride">acetic anhydride</a>.  These processes include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monsanto_acetic_acid_synthesis" title="Monsanto acetic acid synthesis">Monsanto acetic acid synthesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cativa_process" title="Cativa process">Cativa process</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Monsanto_process#Tennessee_Eastman_acetic_anhydride_process" title="Monsanto process">Tennessee Eastman acetic anhydride process</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Methanol_(data_page)" title="Methanol (data page)">Methanol (data page)</a></li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Boyle discusses the distillation of liquids from the wood of the box shrub in:  Robert Boyle, <i>The Sceptical Chymist</i> (London, England:  J. Cadwell, 1661), <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/scepticalchymis00BoylA#page/192/mode/2up/" rel="nofollow">pp. 192–195</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Dumas-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Dumas_54-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Dumas_54-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">A report on methanol to the French Academy of Sciences by J. Dumas and E. Péligot began during the Academy's meeting of 27 October 1834 and finished during the meeting of 3 November 1834.  See:  <i>Procès-verbaux des séances de l'Académie</i>, <b>10</b> :  600–601.  Available on:  <a class="external text" href="http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/bpt6k33037/f606.image" rel="nofollow">Gallica</a>.  The complete report appears in:  J. Dumas and E. Péligot (1835) <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=xSs8oDQV4uYC&amp;&amp;pg=PA44" rel="nofollow">"Mémoire sur l'espirit de bois et sur les divers composés ethérés qui en proviennent"</a> (Memoir on spirit of wood and on the various ethereal compounds that derive therefrom), <i>Annales de chimie et de physique</i>, <b>58</b> : 5–74; from <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=94c5AAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA9" rel="nofollow">page 9</a>:  <i>Nous donnerons le nom de </i>méthylène<i> (1) à un radical …  (1) Μεθυ, vin, et υλη, bois; c'est-à-dire vin ou liqueur spiritueuse du bois.</i> (We will give the name methylene (1) to a radical …  (1) <i>methy</i>, wine, and <i>hulē</i>, wood; that is, wine or spirit of wood.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a report on the International Conference on Chemical Nomenclature that was held in April 1892 in Geneva, Switzerland, see:

<li>Armstrong's report is reprinted with the resolutions in English in: <cite class="citation journal">Armstrong, Henry (1892). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=RogMAQAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA398" rel="nofollow">"The International Conference on Chemical Nomenclature"</a>. <i>The Journal of Analytical and Applied Chemistry</i>. <b>6</b> (1177): 390–400. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1892Natur..46...56A" rel="nofollow">1892Natur..46...56A</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1038%2F046056c0" rel="nofollow">10.1038/046056c0</a>. <q>p. 398: 15.
